<p><b>Bees</b> are flying <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> closely related to <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, known for their role in <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> and, in the case of the best-known bee species, the <a href="/wiki/Western_honey_bee" title="Western honey bee">western honey bee</a>, for producing <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">beeswax</a>. Bees are a <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a> lineage within the superfamily <a href="/wiki/Apoidea" title="Apoidea">Apoidea</a> and are presently considered a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, called <b>Anthophila</b>. There are over 16,000 known species of bees in seven recognized <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">biological families</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Danforthetal2006_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Danforthetal2006-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michener2000_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michener2000-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>  They are found on every continent except <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, in every habitat on the planet that contains insect-pollinated <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>.
</p><p>Some species - including <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">stingless bees</a> - live <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">socially</a> in colonies. Bees are adapted for feeding on <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, the former primarily as an energy source and the latter primarily for <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and other nutrients. Most pollen is used as food for <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a>. Bee pollination is important both ecologically and <a href="/wiki/List_of_crop_plants_pollinated_by_bees" title="List of crop plants pollinated by bees">commercially</a>. The decline in wild bees has increased the value of pollination by commercially managed hives of honey bees.
</p><p>Bees range in size from tiny <a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">stingless bee</a> species whose workers are less than 2 millimetres (0.08&#160;in) long, to <i><a href="/wiki/Megachile_pluto" title="Megachile pluto">Megachile pluto</a></i>, the largest species of <a href="/wiki/Megachilidae" title="Megachilidae">leafcutter bee</a>, whose females can attain a length of 39 millimetres (1.54&#160;in). The most common bees in the Northern Hemisphere are the <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">Halictidae</a>, or sweat bees, but they are small and often mistaken for wasps or flies. Vertebrate predators of bees include birds such as <a href="/wiki/Bee-eater" title="Bee-eater">bee-eaters</a>; insect predators include <a href="/wiki/Beewolf" title="Beewolf">beewolves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>.
</p><p>Human <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">beekeeping</a> or apiculture has been practised for millennia, since at least the times of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>. Apart from honey and pollination, honey bees produce beeswax, <a href="/wiki/Royal_jelly" title="Royal jelly">royal jelly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Propolis" title="Propolis">propolis</a>. Bees have appeared in mythology and folklore, through all phases of art and literature, from ancient times to the present day, though primarily focused in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, where beekeeping is far more common.
</p><p>The analysis of 353 wild bee and hoverfly species  across Britain from 1980 to 2013 found the insects have been lost from a quarter of the places they inhabited in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg/220px-Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg/330px-Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Melittosphex_burmensis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Melittosphex_burmensis" title="Melittosphex burmensis">Melittosphex burmensis</a></i>, a fossil bee preserved in <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cretaceous" title="Early Cretaceous">Early Cretaceous</a> (100 to 145 million years ago) of <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a></div></div></div>
<p>The ancestors of bees were <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a> in the family <a href="/wiki/Crabronidae" title="Crabronidae">Crabronidae</a>, which were <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a> of other insects. The switch from insect prey to pollen may have resulted from the consumption of prey insects which were flower visitors and were partially covered with pollen when they were fed to the wasp larvae. This same <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> scenario may have occurred within the <a href="/wiki/Vespoidea" title="Vespoidea">vespoid</a> wasps, where the <a href="/wiki/Pollen_wasp" title="Pollen wasp">pollen wasps</a> evolved from predatory ancestors. Until recently, the oldest non-compression bee fossil had been found in New Jersey <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>, <i>Cretotrigona prisca</i> of <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous age</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Apinae" title="Apinae">corbiculate bee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A bee fossil from the <a href="/wiki/Early_Cretaceous" title="Early Cretaceous">early Cretaceous</a> (~100 mya), <i><a href="/wiki/Melittosphex_burmensis" title="Melittosphex burmensis">Melittosphex burmensis</a></i>, is considered <i>"an extinct lineage of pollen-collecting Apoidea <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">sister</a> to the modern bees"</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Poinar_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poinar-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Derived features of its morphology (<a href="/wiki/Synapomorphy_and_apomorphy" title="Synapomorphy and apomorphy">apomorphies</a>) place it clearly within the bees, but it retains two unmodified ancestral traits (<a href="/wiki/Plesiomorphy_and_symplesiomorphy" title="Plesiomorphy and symplesiomorphy">plesiomorphies</a>) of the legs (two mid-tibial spurs, and a slender hind basitarsus), showing its transitional status.<sup id="cite_ref-Poinar_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poinar-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> (~45 mya) there was already considerable diversity among eusocial bee lineages.<sup id="cite_ref-pnas3054932_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pnas3054932-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The highly <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial</a> <a href="/wiki/Apinae" title="Apinae">corbiculate Apidae</a> appeared roughly 87 Mya, and the <a href="/wiki/Allodapini" title="Allodapini">Allodapini</a> (within the Apidae) around 53 Mya.<sup id="cite_ref-danforth2012_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-danforth2012-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Colletidae" title="Colletidae">Colletidae</a> appear as fossils only from the late <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> (~25 Mya) to early <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Melittidae" title="Melittidae">Melittidae</a> are known from <i>Palaeomacropis eocenicus</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Ypresian" title="Ypresian">Early Eocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Megachilidae" title="Megachilidae">Megachilidae</a> are known from trace fossils (characteristic leaf cuttings) from the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Middle Eocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Andrenidae" title="Andrenidae">Andrenidae</a> are known from the Eocene-Oligocene boundary, around 34 Mya, of the Florissant shale.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">Halictidae</a> first appear in the Early Eocene<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> with species<sup id="cite_ref-Engel1995_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel1995-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Engel2000_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel2000-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> found in amber. The <a href="/wiki/Stenotritidae" title="Stenotritidae">Stenotritidae</a> are known from fossil brood cells of <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> age.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coevolution">Coevolution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg/200px-Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg/300px-Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg/400px-Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1384" data-file-height="1543" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amegilla_cingulata_on_long_tube_of_Acanthus_ilicifolius_flower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-tongued bees and long-tubed flowers <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolved</a>, like this <i><a href="/wiki/Amegilla_cingulata" title="Amegilla cingulata">Amegilla cingulata</a></i> (Apidae) on <i><a href="/wiki/Acanthus_ilicifolius" title="Acanthus ilicifolius">Acanthus ilicifolius</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">Coevolution</a></div>
<p>The earliest animal-pollinated flowers were shallow, cup-shaped blooms <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinated</a> by insects such as <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, so the <a href="/wiki/Pollination_syndrome" title="Pollination syndrome">syndrome of insect pollination</a> was well established before the first appearance of bees. The novelty is that bees are <a href="/wiki/Specialization_in_bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Specialization in bees">specialized</a> as pollination agents, with behavioral and physical modifications that specifically enhance pollination, and are the most efficient pollinating insects. In a process of <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolution</a>, flowers developed floral rewards<sup id="cite_ref-Armbruster_ch._3_Evol._Plant-pollinator_relationships_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armbruster_ch._3_Evol._Plant-pollinator_relationships-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> and longer tubes, and bees developed longer tongues to extract the nectar.<sup id="cite_ref-Michener1974_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michener1974-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Bees also developed structures known as <a href="/wiki/Scopa_(biology)" title="Scopa (biology)">scopal hairs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollen_baskets" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollen baskets">pollen baskets</a> to collect and carry pollen. The location and type differ among and between groups of bees. Most bees have <a href="/wiki/Scopa_(biology)" title="Scopa (biology)">scopal hairs</a> located on their hind legs or on the underside of their abdomens, some bees in the family Apidae possess <a href="/wiki/Pollen_baskets" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollen baskets">pollen baskets</a> on their hind legs while very few species lack these entirely and instead collect pollen in their crops.<sup id="cite_ref-Michener2000_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michener2000-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This drove the <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">angiosperms</a>, and, in turn, the bees themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchmann2012_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchmann2012-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Bees have not only coevolved with flowers but it is believed that some bees have coevolved with mites. Some bees provide tufts of hairs called acarinaria that appear to provide lodgings for mites; in return, it is believed that the mites eat fungi that attack pollen, so the relationship in this case may be <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistc</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavel_B_2007_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavel_B_2007-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="External">External</span></h4>
<p>This <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> is based on Debevic <i>et al</i>, 2012, which used molecular phylogeny to demonstrate that the bees (<a href="/wiki/Anthophila" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthophila">Anthophila</a>) arose from deep within the Crabronidae, which is therefore <a href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>. The placement of the <a href="/wiki/Heterogynaidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterogynaidae">Heterogynaidae</a> is uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The small subfamily <a href="/wiki/Mellininae" title="Mellininae">Mellininae</a> was not included in their analysis.
</p><p><br />
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal">Internal</span></h4>
<p>This cladogram of the bee families is based on Hedtke et al., 2013, which places the former families Dasypodaidae and Meganomiidae as subfamilies inside the Melittidae.<sup id="cite_ref-Hedtke2013_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hedtke2013-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
English names, where available, are given in parentheses.
</p><p><br />

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Honeybee_(Apis_mellifera)_lapping_mouthparts,_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg/170px-European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg/255px-European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg/340px-European_Honeybee_%28Apis_mellifera%29_lapping_mouthparts%2C_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="2989" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Honeybee_(Apis_mellifera)_lapping_mouthparts,_showing_labium_and_maxillae..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lapping <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">mouthparts</a> of a honey bee, showing labium and maxillae</div></div></div>
<p>Bees differ from closely related groups such as wasps by having branched or plume-like <a href="/wiki/Seta" title="Seta">setae</a> (hairs), combs on the forelimbs for cleaning their antennae, small anatomical differences in the limb structure, and the venation of the hind wings; and in females, by having the seventh dorsal abdominal plate divided into two half-plates.<sup id="cite_ref-Grimaldi_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grimaldi-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bees have the following characteristics:
</p>
<ul><li>A pair of large <a href="/wiki/Compound_eyes" class="mw-redirect" title="Compound eyes">compound eyes</a> which cover much of the surface of the head. Between and above these are three small simple eyes (<a href="/wiki/Ocelli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a>) which provide information on light intensity.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> usually have thirteen segments in males and twelve in females, and are <a href="/wiki/Insect_morphology#Antennae" title="Insect morphology">geniculate</a>, having an elbow joint part way along. They house large numbers of sense organs that can detect touch (mechanoreceptors), smell and taste; and small, hairlike mechanoreceptors that can detect air movement so as to "hear" sounds.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Insect_mouthparts" title="Insect mouthparts">mouthparts</a> are adapted for both chewing and sucking by having both a pair of <a href="/wiki/Mandible_(insect_mouthpart)" title="Mandible (insect mouthpart)">mandibles</a> and a long <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a> for sucking up nectar.<sup id="cite_ref-Extension_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The thorax has three segments, each with a pair of robust legs, and a pair of membranous wings on the hind two segments. The front legs of corbiculate bees bear combs for cleaning the antennae, and in many species the hind legs bear pollen baskets, flattened sections with incurving hairs to secure the collected pollen. The wings are synchronised in flight, and the somewhat smaller hind wings connect to the forewings by a row of hooks along their margin which connect to a groove in the forewing.</li>
<li>The abdomen has nine segments, the hindermost three being modified into the sting.<sup id="cite_ref-Extension_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Extension-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg/220px-Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg/330px-Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg/440px-Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carpenter_bee_head_and_compound_eyes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head-on view of a <a href="/wiki/Carpenter_bee" title="Carpenter bee">carpenter bee</a>, showing antennae, three <a href="/wiki/Ocelli" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocelli">ocelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compound_eye" title="Compound eye">compound eyes</a>, sensory bristles and mouthparts</div></div></div>
<p>The world's largest species of bee is thought to be Wallace's giant bee <i><a href="/wiki/Megachile_pluto" title="Megachile pluto">Megachile pluto</a></i>, whose females can attain a length of 39 millimetres (1.54&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The smallest species may be dwarf stingless bees in the tribe <a href="/wiki/Meliponini" class="mw-redirect" title="Meliponini">Meliponini</a> whose workers are less than 2 millimetres (0.08&#160;in) in length.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociality">Sociality</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haplodiploid_breeding_system">Haplodiploid breeding system</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploidy" title="Haplodiploidy">Haplodiploidy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wasp_attack.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Wasp_attack.jpg/290px-Wasp_attack.jpg" decoding="async" width="290" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Wasp_attack.jpg/435px-Wasp_attack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Wasp_attack.jpg/580px-Wasp_attack.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2292" data-file-height="1212" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wasp_attack.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Willing to die for their sisters: worker honey bees killed defending their hive against <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, along with a dead wasp. Such <a href="/wiki/Altruism" title="Altruism">altruistic</a> behaviour may be favoured by the <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplodiploid">haplodiploid</a> <a href="/wiki/Sex-determination_system" title="Sex-determination system">sex determination</a> system of bees.</div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Inclusive_fitness" title="Inclusive fitness">inclusive fitness</a> theory, organisms can gain fitness not just through increasing their own reproductive output, but also that of close relatives. In evolutionary terms, individuals should help relatives when <i>Cost &lt; Relatedness * Benefit</i>. The requirements for eusociality are more easily fulfilled by <a href="/wiki/Haplodiploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplodiploid">haplodiploid</a> species such as bees because of their unusual relatedness structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes2008_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2008-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
In haplodiploid species, females develop from fertilized eggs and males from unfertilized eggs. Because a male is <a href="/wiki/Haploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Haploid">haploid</a> (has only one copy of each gene), his daughters (which are <a href="/wiki/Diploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Diploid">diploid</a>, with two copies of each gene) share 100% of his genes and 50% of their mother's. Therefore, they share 75% of their genes with each other. This mechanism of sex determination gives rise to what <a href="/wiki/W._D._Hamilton" title="W. D. Hamilton">W. D. Hamilton</a> termed "supersisters", more closely related to their sisters than they would be to their own offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamilton1964II_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton1964II-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Workers often do not reproduce, but they can pass on more of their genes by helping to raise their sisters (as queens) than they would by having their own offspring (each of which would only have 50% of their genes), assuming they would produce similar numbers. This unusual situation has been proposed as an explanation of the multiple independent evolutions of eusociality (arising at least nine separate times) within the <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
However, some eusocial species such as <a href="/wiki/Termites" class="mw-redirect" title="Termites">termites</a> are not haplodiploid. Conversely, all bees are haplodiploid but not all are eusocial, and among eusocial species many queens mate with multiple males, creating half-sisters that share only 25% of their genes.<sup id="cite_ref-NTW_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTW-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Haplodiploidy is thus neither necessary nor sufficient for eusociality. But, monogamy (queens mating singly) is the ancestral state for all eusocial species so far investigated, so it is likely that haplodiploidy contributed to the evolution of eusociality in bees.<sup id="cite_ref-Hughes_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eusociality">Eusociality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg/170px-Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg/255px-Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg/340px-Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_swarm_on_fallen_tree03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bee</a> swarm</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">Eusociality</a></div>
<p>Bees may be solitary or may live in various types of communities. <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">Eusociality</a> appears to have originated from at least three independent origins in halictid bees.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The most advanced of these are species with <a href="/wiki/Eusociality" title="Eusociality">eusocial</a> colonies; these are characterised by having cooperative brood care and a division of labour into reproductive and non-reproductive adults, plus overlapping generations.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This division of labour creates specialized groups within eusocial societies which are called <a href="/wiki/Eusociality#In_insects" title="Eusociality">castes</a>. In some species, groups of cohabiting females may be sisters, and if there is a <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a> within the group, they are considered <a href="/wiki/Semisocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Semisocial">semisocial</a>. The group is called eusocial if, in addition, the group consists of a mother (the <a href="/wiki/Queen_bee" title="Queen bee">queen</a>) and her daughters (<a href="/wiki/Worker_bee" title="Worker bee">workers</a>). When the castes are purely behavioural alternatives, with no morphological differentiation other than size, the system is considered primitively eusocial, as in many <a href="/wiki/Paper_wasp" title="Paper wasp">paper wasps</a>; when the castes are morphologically discrete, the system is considered highly eusocial.<sup id="cite_ref-Michener1974_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michener1974-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The true honey bees (genus <i><a href="/wiki/Apis_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apis (genus)">Apis</a></i>, of which there are seven currently-recognized species) are highly eusocial, and are among the best known of all insects. Their colonies are established by <a href="/wiki/Swarming_(honey_bee)" title="Swarming (honey bee)">swarms</a>, consisting of a queen and several hundred workers. There are 29 subspecies of one of these species, <i><a href="/wiki/Apis_mellifera" class="mw-redirect" title="Apis mellifera">Apis mellifera</a></i>, native to Europe, the Middle East, and Africa. <a href="/wiki/Africanized_bee" title="Africanized bee">Africanized bees</a> are a hybrid strain of <i>A. mellifera</i> that escaped from experiments involving crossing European and African subspecies; they are extremely defensive.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stingless_bee" title="Stingless bee">Stingless bees</a> are also highly <a href="/wiki/Eusocial" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusocial">eusocial</a>. They practise <a href="/wiki/Mass_provisioning" title="Mass provisioning">mass provisioning</a>, with complex nest architecture and perennial colonies also established via swarming.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bumblebee_05.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bumblebee_05.JPG/220px-Bumblebee_05.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bumblebee_05.JPG/330px-Bumblebee_05.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Bumblebee_05.JPG/440px-Bumblebee_05.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bumblebee_05.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bumblebee" title="Bumblebee">bumblebee</a> carrying pollen in its <a href="/wiki/Pollen_basket" title="Pollen basket">pollen baskets</a> (corbiculae)</div></div></div>
<p>Many bumblebees are eusocial, similar to the eusocial <a href="/wiki/Vespidae" title="Vespidae">Vespidae</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hornet" title="Hornet">hornets</a> in that the queen initiates a nest on her own rather than by swarming. Bumblebee colonies typically have from 50 to 200 bees at peak population, which occurs in mid to late summer. Nest architecture is simple, limited by the size of the pre-existing nest cavity, and colonies rarely last more than a year.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCT-nest_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCT-nest-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> set up the Bumblebee Specialist Group to review the threat status of all bumblebee species worldwide using the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> criteria.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many more species of primitively eusocial than highly eusocial bees, but they have been studied less often. Most are in the family <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">Halictidae</a>, or "sweat bees". Colonies are typically small, with a dozen or fewer workers, on average. Queens and workers differ only in size, if at all. Most species have a single season colony cycle, even in the tropics, and only mated females hibernate. A few species have long active seasons and attain colony sizes in the hundreds, such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Halictus_hesperus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Halictus hesperus (page does not exist)">Halictus hesperus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Some species are eusocial in parts of their range and solitary in others,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> or have a mix of eusocial and solitary nests in the same population.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Orchid_bee" class="mw-redirect" title="Orchid bee">orchid bees</a> (Apidae) include some primitively eusocial species with similar biology. Some <a href="/wiki/Allodapini" title="Allodapini">allodapine</a> bees (Apidae) form primitively eusocial colonies, with <a href="/wiki/Progressive_provisioning" title="Progressive provisioning">progressive provisioning</a>: a larva's food is supplied gradually as it develops, as is the case in honey bees and some bumblebees.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solitary_and_communal_bees">Solitary and communal bees</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Megachile_rotundata.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Megachile_rotundata.JPG/220px-Megachile_rotundata.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Megachile_rotundata.JPG/330px-Megachile_rotundata.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Megachile_rotundata.JPG/440px-Megachile_rotundata.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Megachile_rotundata.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A leafcutting bee, <i><a href="/wiki/Megachile_rotundata" title="Megachile rotundata">Megachile rotundata</a></i>, cutting circles from acacia leaves</div></div></div>
<p>Most other bees, including familiar insects such as <a href="/wiki/Carpenter_bee" title="Carpenter bee">carpenter bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leafcutter_bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Leafcutter bees">leafcutter bees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mason_bees" class="mw-redirect" title="Mason bees">mason bees</a> are solitary in the sense that every female is fertile, and typically inhabits a nest she constructs herself. There is no division of labor so these nests lack queens and <i>worker</i> bees for these species. Solitary bees typically produce neither honey nor <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">beeswax</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Paetzel_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paetzel-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Bees collect pollen to feed their young, and have the necessary adaptations to do this. However, certain wasp species such as <a href="/wiki/Pollen_wasp" title="Pollen wasp">pollen wasps</a> have similar behaviours, and a few species of bee <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenge</a> from carcases to feed their offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-Grimaldi_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grimaldi-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Solitary bees are important pollinators; they gather pollen to provision their nests with food for their brood. Often it is mixed with nectar to form a paste-like consistency. Some solitary bees have advanced types of pollen-carrying structures on their bodies. A very few species of solitary bees are being cultured for commercial pollination. Most of these species belong to a distinct set of <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> which are commonly known by their nesting behavior or preferences, namely: carpenter bees, <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">sweat bees</a>, mason bees, <a href="/wiki/Colletes_inaequalis" title="Colletes inaequalis">plasterer bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squash_bee" title="Squash bee">squash bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceratina" title="Ceratina">dwarf carpenter bees</a>, leafcutter bees, <a href="/wiki/Alkali_bee" title="Alkali bee">alkali bees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthophorini" title="Anthophorini">digger bees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg/220px-Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg/330px-Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg/440px-Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1857" data-file-height="1486" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthidium_February_2008-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A solitary bee, <i><a href="/wiki/Anthidium_florentinum" title="Anthidium florentinum">Anthidium florentinum</a></i> (family <a href="/wiki/Megachilidae" title="Megachilidae">Megachilidae</a>), visiting <i><a href="/wiki/Lantana" title="Lantana">Lantana</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Most solitary bees nest in the ground in a variety of soil textures and conditions while others create nests in hollow <a href="/wiki/Phragmites" title="Phragmites">reeds</a> or twigs, holes in <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>. The female typically creates a compartment (a "cell") with an egg and some provisions for the resulting larva, then seals it off. A nest may consist of numerous cells. When the nest is in wood, usually the last (those closer to the entrance) contain eggs that will become males. The adult does not provide care for the brood once the egg is laid, and usually dies after making one or more nests. The males typically emerge first and are ready for mating when the females emerge. Solitary bees are either stingless or very unlikely to sting (only in self-defense, if ever).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg/220px-Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg/330px-Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg/440px-Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Osmia_cornifrons.5.1.08.w.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mason_bee" title="Mason bee">mason bee</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Osmia_cornifrons" title="Osmia cornifrons">Osmia cornifrons</a></i> nests in a hole in dead wood. <a href="/wiki/Insect_hotel#Solitary_bees_and_wasps" title="Insect hotel">Bee "hotels"</a> are often sold for this purpose.</div></div></div>
<p>While solitary, females each make individual nests. Some species, such as the European mason bee <i><a href="/wiki/Hoplitis_anthocopoides" title="Hoplitis anthocopoides">Hoplitis anthocopoides</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Eickwort_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eickwort-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Amegilla_dawsoni" title="Amegilla dawsoni">Dawson's Burrowing bee</a>, <i>Amegilla dawsoni,</i><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> are gregarious, preferring to make nests near others of the same species, and giving the appearance of being social. Large groups of solitary bee nests are called <i>aggregations</i>, to distinguish them from <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a>. In some species, multiple females share a common nest, but each makes and provisions her own cells independently. This type of group is called "communal" and is not uncommon. The primary advantage appears to be that a nest entrance is easier to defend from predators and parasites when there are multiple females using that same entrance on a regular basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Eickwort_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eickwort-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee_life_cycle" title="Honey bee life cycle">Honey bee life cycle</a></div>
<p>The life cycle of a bee, be it a solitary or social species, involves the laying of an egg, the development through several moults of a legless <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larva</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupation</a> stage during which the insect undergoes <a href="/wiki/Holometabolism" title="Holometabolism">complete metamorphosis</a>, followed by the emergence of a winged adult. Most solitary bees and bumble bees in temperate climates overwinter as adults or pupae and emerge in spring when increasing numbers of flowering plants come into bloom. The males usually emerge first and search for females with which to mate. The sex of a bee is determined by whether or not the egg is fertilised; after mating, a female stores the sperm, and determines which sex is required at the time each individual egg is laid, fertilised eggs producing female offspring and unfertilised eggs, males. Tropical bees may have several generations in a year and no <a href="/wiki/Diapause" title="Diapause">diapause</a> stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Roubik1992_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roubik1992-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The egg is generally oblong, slightly curved and tapering at one end. In the case of solitary bees, each one is laid in a cell with a supply of mixed pollen and nectar next to it. This may be rolled into a pellet or placed in a pile and is known as mass provisioning. In social species of bee there is progressive provisioning with the larva being fed regularly while it grows. The nest varies from a hole in the ground or in wood, in solitary bees, to a substantial structure with wax combs in bumblebees and honey bees.<sup id="cite_ref-Shuckard_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuckard-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The larvae are generally whitish grubs, roughly oval and bluntly-pointed at both ends. They have fifteen segments and <a href="/wiki/Spiracle_(arthropods)" title="Spiracle (arthropods)">spiracles</a> in each segment for breathing. They have no legs but are able to move within the confines of the cell, helped by tubercles on their sides. They have short horns on the head, jaws for chewing their food and an appendage on either side of the mouth tipped with a bristle. There is a gland under the mouth that secretes a viscous liquid which solidifies into the silk they use to produce their cocoons. The pupa can be seen through the semi-transparent cocoon and over the course of a few days, the insect undergoes metamorphosis into the form of the adult bee. When ready to emerge, it splits its skin dorsally and climbs out of the <a href="/wiki/Exuvia" class="mw-redirect" title="Exuvia">exuviae</a> as a winged adult and breaks out of the cell.<sup id="cite_ref-Shuckard_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuckard-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<center><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
