<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">Flight</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/220px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/330px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg/440px-Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1754" data-file-height="1169" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apis_mellifera_flying.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Honeybee in flight carrying pollen in <a href="/wiki/Pollen_basket" title="Pollen basket">pollen basket</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Insect_flight" title="Insect flight">Insect flight</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Magnan" title="Antoine Magnan">Antoine Magnan</a>'s 1934 book <i>Le vol des insectes</i>, he wrote that he and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Sainte-Lagu%C3%AB" title="André Sainte-Laguë">André Sainte-Laguë</a> had applied the equations of <a href="/wiki/Air_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a> to <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and found that their flight could not be explained by fixed-wing calculations, but that "One shouldn't be surprised that the results of the calculations don't square with reality".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This has led to a common misconception that bees "violate aerodynamic theory", but in fact it merely confirms that bees do not engage in fixed-wing flight, and that their flight is explained by other mechanics, such as those used by <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">helicopters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 1996 it was shown that vortices created by many insects' wings helped to provide lift.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> High-speed <a href="/wiki/Cinematography" title="Cinematography">cinematography</a><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and robotic mock-up of a bee wing<sup id="cite_ref-caltech_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caltech-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> showed that lift was generated by "the unconventional combination of short, choppy wing strokes, a rapid rotation of the wing as it flops over and reverses direction, and a very fast wing-beat frequency". Wing-beat frequency normally increases as size decreases, but as the bee's wing beat covers such a small <a href="/wiki/Arc_(geometry)" title="Arc (geometry)">arc</a>, it flaps approximately 230 times per second, faster than a <a href="/wiki/Drosophilidae" title="Drosophilidae">fruitfly</a> (200 times per second) which is 80 times smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Navigation.2C_communication.2C_and_finding_food"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation,_communication,_and_finding_food">Navigation, communication, and finding food</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_dance.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bee_dance.svg/170px-Bee_dance.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bee_dance.svg/255px-Bee_dance.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Bee_dance.svg/340px-Bee_dance.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="598" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bee_dance.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Karl_von_Frisch" title="Karl von Frisch">Karl von Frisch</a> (1953) discovered that honey bee workers can <a href="/wiki/Animal_navigation" title="Animal navigation">navigate</a>, indicating the range and direction to food to other workers with a <a href="/wiki/Waggle_dance" title="Waggle dance">waggle dance</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Animal_navigation" title="Animal navigation">Animal navigation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waggle_dance" title="Waggle dance">Waggle dance</a></div>
<p>The ethologist <a href="/wiki/Karl_von_Frisch" title="Karl von Frisch">Karl von Frisch</a> studied <a href="/wiki/Animal_navigation" title="Animal navigation">navigation</a> in the honey bee. He showed that honey bees communicate by the <a href="/wiki/Waggle_dance" title="Waggle dance">waggle dance</a>, in which a worker indicates the location of a food source to other workers in the hive. He demonstrated that bees can recognize a desired compass direction in three different ways: by the sun, by the <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a> pattern of the blue sky, and by the earth's magnetic field. He showed that the sun is the preferred or main compass; the other mechanisms are used under cloudy skies or inside a dark <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">beehive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-vonFrisch93_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vonFrisch93-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Bees navigate using spatial memory with a "rich, map-like organization".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floral_relationships">Floral relationships</span></h3>
<p>Most bees are polylectic (generalist) meaning they collect pollen from a range of flowering plants, but some are <a href="/wiki/Oligolege" class="mw-redirect" title="Oligolege">oligoleges</a> (specialists), in that they only gather pollen from one or a few species or genera of closely related plants.<sup id="cite_ref-Waser2006_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waser2006-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Specialist pollinators also include bee species which gather floral oils instead of pollen, and male orchid bees, which gather aromatic compounds from <a href="/wiki/Orchid" class="mw-redirect" title="Orchid">orchids</a> (one of the few cases where male bees are effective pollinators). Bees are able to sense the presence of desirable flowers through ultraviolet patterning on flowers, floral odors,<sup id="cite_ref-Dafni_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dafni-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and even electromagnetic fields.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Once landed, a bee then uses nectar quality<sup id="cite_ref-Dafni_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dafni-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and pollen taste<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> to determine whether to continue visiting similar flowers.
</p><p>In rare cases, a <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> species may only be effectively pollinated by a single bee species, and some plants are <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> at least in part because their pollinator is also threatened. There is, however, a pronounced tendency for oligolectic bees to be associated with common, widespread plants which are visited by multiple pollinators. There are some forty oligoleges associated with the <a href="/wiki/Larrea_tridentata" title="Larrea tridentata">creosote bush</a> in the arid parts of the United States southwest, for example.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_mimics_and_models">As mimics and models</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg/220px-Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg/330px-Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg/440px-Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1099" data-file-height="985" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bombylius_major_on_flower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bee-fly <i><a href="/wiki/Bombylius_major" title="Bombylius major">Bombylius major</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimic" class="mw-redirect" title="Batesian mimic">Batesian mimic</a> of bees, taking nectar and pollinating a flower.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">Mimicry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">Batesian mimicry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCllerian_mimicry" title="Müllerian mimicry">Müllerian mimicry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/170px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/255px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/340px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="745" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ophrys_apifera" title="Ophrys apifera">Bee orchid</a> lures male bees to attempt to mate with the flower's lip, which resembles a bee perched on a pink flower.</div></div></div>
<p>Many bees are <a href="/wiki/Aposematic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aposematic">aposematically</a> coloured, typically orange and black, warning of their ability to defend themselves with a powerful sting. As such they are models for <a href="/wiki/Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry">Batesian mimicry</a> by non-stinging insects such as <a href="/wiki/Bee-flies" class="mw-redirect" title="Bee-flies">bee-flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asilidae" title="Asilidae">robber flies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hoverflies" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoverflies">hoverflies</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ThorpHorning1983_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThorpHorning1983-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> all of which gain a measure of protection by superficially looking and behaving like bees.<sup id="cite_ref-ThorpHorning1983_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThorpHorning1983-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bees are themselves <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCllerian_mimicry" title="Müllerian mimicry">Müllerian mimics</a> of other aposematic insects with the same colour scheme, including <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycidae" title="Lycidae">lycid</a> and other beetles, and many butterflies and moths (<a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>) which are themselves distasteful, often through acquiring bitter and poisonous chemicals from their plant food. All the Müllerian mimics, including bees, benefit from the reduced risk of predation that results from their easily recognised warning coloration.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bees are also mimicked by plants such as the <a href="/wiki/Ophrys_apifera" title="Ophrys apifera">bee orchid</a> which imitates both the appearance and the scent of a female bee; male bees attempt to mate (<a href="/wiki/Pseudocopulation" title="Pseudocopulation">pseudocopulation</a>) with the furry lip of the flower, thus pollinating it.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_brood_parasites">As brood parasites</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg/220px-Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg/330px-Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg/440px-Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2397" data-file-height="2275" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bumblebee_January_2008-4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Bombus_vestalis" title="Bombus vestalis">Bombus vestalis</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasite</a> of the bumblebee <i><a href="/wiki/Bombus_terrestris" title="Bombus terrestris">Bombus terrestris</a></i></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">Brood parasite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nest_usurpation" title="Nest usurpation">Nest usurpation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">Brood parasites</a> occur in several bee families including the <a href="/wiki/Apidae" title="Apidae">apid</a> subfamily <a href="/wiki/Nomadinae" title="Nomadinae">Nomadinae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Females of these bees lack pollen collecting structures (the <a href="/wiki/Scopa_(biology)" title="Scopa (biology)">scopa</a>) and do not construct their own nests. They typically enter the nests of pollen collecting species, and lay their eggs in cells provisioned by the host bee. When the cuckoo bee larva hatches it consumes the host larva's pollen ball, and often the host egg also.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Arctic bee species, <i><a href="/wiki/Bombus_hyperboreus" title="Bombus hyperboreus">Bombus hyperboreus</a>,</i> in particular are an aggressive species that attack and enslave other bees of the same subgenus. However, unlike many other bee brood parasites, they have pollen baskets and often collect pollen.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the south of Africa, hives of African honeybees (<i>A. mellifera scutellata</i>) are being destroyed by parasitic workers of the Cape honeybee, <i>A. m. capensis</i>. These lay <a href="/wiki/Diploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Diploid">diploid</a> eggs ("<a href="/wiki/Thelytoky" title="Thelytoky">thelytoky</a>"), escaping normal <a href="/wiki/Worker_policing" title="Worker policing">worker policing</a>, leading to the colony's destruction; the parasites can then move to other hives.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo_bee" title="Cuckoo bee">cuckoo bees</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_bumblebee_species" title="List of bumblebee species">Bombus</a></i> subgenus <i>Psithyrus</i> are closely related to, and resemble, their hosts in looks and size. This common pattern gave rise to the ecological principle "<a href="/wiki/Emery%27s_rule" title="Emery&#39;s rule">Emery's rule</a>". Others parasitize bees in different families, like <i><a href="/wiki/Townsendiella" title="Townsendiella">Townsendiella</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Nomadinae" title="Nomadinae">nomadine</a> <a href="/wiki/Apidae" title="Apidae">apid</a>, two species of which are cleptoparasites of the <a href="/wiki/Dasypodaidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Dasypodaidae">dasypodaid</a> genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hesperapis" title="Hesperapis">Hesperapis</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> while the other species in the same genus attacks <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">halictid</a> bees.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nocturnal_bees">Nocturnal bees</span></h3>
<p>Four bee families (<a href="/wiki/Andrenidae" title="Andrenidae">Andrenidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colletidae" title="Colletidae">Colletidae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halictidae" title="Halictidae">Halictidae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apidae" title="Apidae">Apidae</a>) contain some species that are <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a>. Most are tropical or subtropical, but there are some which live in arid regions at higher latitudes. These bees have greatly enlarged <a href="/wiki/Ocellus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocellus">ocelli</a>, which are extremely sensitive to light and dark, though incapable of forming images. Some have refracting superposition compound eyes: these combine the output of many elements of their compound eyes to provide enough light for each retinal photoreceptor. Their ability to fly by night enables them to avoid many predators, and to exploit flowers that produce nectar only or also at night.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Predators.2C_parasites_and_pathogens"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators,_parasites_and_pathogens">Predators, parasites and pathogens</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Diseases_of_the_honey_bee" class="mw-redirect" title="Diseases of the honey bee">Diseases of the honey bee</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg/220px-Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg/330px-Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg/440px-Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="2097" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pair_of_Merops_apiaster_feeding_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bee-eater, <i><a href="/wiki/Merops_apiaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Merops apiaster">Merops apiaster</a></i>, specialises in feeding on bees; here a male catches a nuptial gift for his mate.</div></div></div>
<p>Vertebrate predators of bees include <a href="/wiki/Bee-eater" title="Bee-eater">bee-eaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrikes" class="mw-redirect" title="Shrikes">shrikes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_flycatcher" title="Old World flycatcher">flycatchers</a>, which make short sallies to catch insects in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-ChittkaThomson2001_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChittkaThomson2001-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Swifts and swallows<sup id="cite_ref-ChittkaThomson2001_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChittkaThomson2001-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> fly almost continually, catching insects as they go. The <a href="/wiki/Pernis_(bird)" title="Pernis (bird)">honey buzzard</a> attacks bees' nests and eats the larvae.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Greater_honeyguide" title="Greater honeyguide">greater honeyguide</a> interacts with humans by guiding them to the nests of wild bees. The humans break open the nests and take the honey and the bird feeds on the larvae and the wax.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Among mammals, predators such as the <a href="/wiki/Badger" title="Badger">badger</a> dig up bumblebee nests and eat both the larvae and any stored food.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg/220px-Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg/330px-Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg/440px-Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2770" data-file-height="2047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wasp_and_bee_August_2008-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The beewolf <i><a href="/wiki/Philanthus_triangulum" class="mw-redirect" title="Philanthus triangulum">Philanthus triangulum</a></i> paralysing a bee with its sting</div></div></div>
<p>Specialist ambush predators of visitors to flowers include <a href="/wiki/Crab_spiders" class="mw-redirect" title="Crab spiders">crab spiders</a>, which wait on flowering plants for pollinating insects; <a href="/wiki/Hemiptera" title="Hemiptera">predatory bugs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Praying_mantis" class="mw-redirect" title="Praying mantis">praying mantises</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ChittkaThomson2001_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChittkaThomson2001-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> some of which (the <a href="/wiki/Flower_mantis" title="Flower mantis">flower mantises</a> of the tropics) wait motionless, <a href="/wiki/Aggressive_mimicry" title="Aggressive mimicry">aggressive mimics</a> camouflaged as flowers.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Beewolf" title="Beewolf">Beewolves</a> are large wasps that habitually attack bees;<sup id="cite_ref-ChittkaThomson2001_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChittkaThomson2001-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ethologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethologist">ethologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Niko_Tinbergen" class="mw-redirect" title="Niko Tinbergen">Niko Tinbergen</a> estimated that a single colony of the beewolf <i><a href="/wiki/Philanthus_triangulum" class="mw-redirect" title="Philanthus triangulum">Philanthus triangulum</a></i> might kill several thousand honeybees in a day: all the prey he observed were honeybees.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Other predatory insects that sometimes catch bees include <a href="/wiki/Asilidae" title="Asilidae">robber flies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ChittkaThomson2001_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChittkaThomson2001-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Honey bees are affected by parasites including <a href="/wiki/Acari" title="Acari">acarine</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Varroa" title="Varroa">Varroa</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> However, some bees are believed to have a mutualistic relationship with mites.<sup id="cite_ref-Pavel_B_2007_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavel_B_2007-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bees_and_humans">Bees and humans</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mythology_and_folklore">In mythology and folklore</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bee_(mythology)" title="Bee (mythology)">Bee (mythology)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg/220px-Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg/330px-Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg/440px-Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1850" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaque_bee-goddess_BM_GR1860.4-123.4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold plaques embossed with winged bee goddesses. <a href="/wiki/Kameiros" class="mw-redirect" title="Kameiros">Camiros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>. 7th century B.C.</div></div></div>
<p>Three bee maidens with the power of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> and thus speaking truth are described in <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hymn_to_Hermes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hymn to Hermes (page does not exist)">Hymn to Hermes</a></i>, and the food of the gods is "identified as honey"; the bee maidens were originally associated with <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>, and are probably not correctly identified with the <a href="/wiki/Thriae" title="Thriae">Thriae</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Honey, according to a Greek myth, was discovered by a nymph called Melissa ("Bee"); and honey was offered to the Greek gods from <a href="/wiki/Helladic_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Helladic period">Mycenean times</a>. Bees were associated, too, with the <a href="/wiki/Delphic_oracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphic oracle">Delphic oracle</a> and the prophetess was sometimes called a bee.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The image of a community of honey bees has been used from ancient to modern times, in <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>; <a href="/wiki/Tolstoy" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolstoy">Tolstoy</a>, and by political and social theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Mandeville" title="Bernard Mandeville">Bernard Mandeville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> as a model for human <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In English folklore, bees would be told of important events in the household, in a custom known as "<a href="/wiki/Telling_the_bees" title="Telling the bees">Telling the bees</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Roud2006_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roud2006-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_art_and_literature">In art and literature</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg/170px-Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg/255px-Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg/340px-Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1646" data-file-height="2020" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mrs_tittlemouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s illustration of Babbity Bumble in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Mrs_Tittlemouse" class="mw-redirect" title="The Tale of Mrs Tittlemouse">The Tale of Mrs Tittlemouse</a></i>, 1910</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the oldest examples of bees in art are <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">rock paintings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> which have been dated to 15,000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-EHSTMNWC_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHSTMNWC-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/W._B._Yeats" title="W. B. Yeats">W. B. Yeats</a>'s poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lake_Isle_of_Innisfree" class="mw-redirect" title="The Lake Isle of Innisfree">The Lake Isle of Innisfree</a></i> (1888) contains the couplet "Nine bean rows will I have there, a hive for the honey bee, / And live alone in the bee loud glade." At the time he was living in <a href="/wiki/Bedford_Park,_London" title="Bedford Park, London">Bedford Park</a> in the West of London.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s illustrated book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Mrs_Tittlemouse" class="mw-redirect" title="The Tale of Mrs Tittlemouse">The Tale of Mrs Tittlemouse</a></i> (1910) features Babbity Bumble and her brood <i>(pictured)</i>. <a href="/wiki/Kit_Williams" title="Kit Williams">Kit Williams</a>' <a href="/wiki/Treasure_hunt_(game)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasure hunt (game)">treasure hunt</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bee_on_the_Comb" title="The Bee on the Comb">The Bee on the Comb</a></i> (1984) uses bees and beekeeping as part of its story and puzzle. <a href="/wiki/Sue_Monk_Kidd" title="Sue Monk Kidd">Sue Monk Kidd</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Bees_(novel)" title="The Secret Life of Bees (novel)">The Secret Life of Bees</a></i> (2004), and the <a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Bees_(film)" title="The Secret Life of Bees (film)">2009 film</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Fanning" title="Dakota Fanning">Dakota Fanning</a>, tells the story of a girl who escapes her abusive home and finds her way to live with a family of beekeepers, the Boatwrights.
</p><p>The humorous 2007 animated film <i><a href="/wiki/Bee_Movie" title="Bee Movie">Bee Movie</a></i> used <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Seinfeld" title="Jerry Seinfeld">Jerry Seinfeld</a>'s first script and was his first work for children; he starred as a bee named Barry B. Benson, alongside <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9e_Zellweger" title="Renée Zellweger">Renée Zellweger</a>. Critics found its premise awkward and its delivery tame.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dave_Goulson" title="Dave Goulson">Dave Goulson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Sting_in_the_Tale" title="A Sting in the Tale">A Sting in the Tale</a></i> (2014) describes his efforts to save bumblebees in Britain, as well as much about their biology. The playwright Laline Paull's <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> <i>The Bees</i> (2015) tells the tale of a hive bee named Flora 717 from hatching onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beekeeping">Beekeeping</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Beekeeping</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beekeeper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beekeeper.jpg/220px-Beekeeper.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beekeeper.jpg/330px-Beekeeper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Beekeeper.jpg/440px-Beekeeper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1388" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beekeeper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A commercial beekeeper at work</div></div></div>
<p>Humans have kept honey bee colonies, commonly in <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">hives</a>, for millennia. <a href="/wiki/Beekeeper" title="Beekeeper">Beekeepers</a> collect <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">beeswax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propolis" title="Propolis">propolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Royal_jelly" title="Royal jelly">royal jelly</a> from hives; bees are also kept to <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinate</a> crops and to produce bees for sale to other beekeepers.
</p><p>Depictions of humans collecting honey from wild bees date to 15,000 years ago; efforts to domesticate them are shown in Egyptian art around 4,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Simple hives and smoke were used;<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bodenheimer1960_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodenheimer1960-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> jars of honey were found in the tombs of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tutankhamun" title="Tutankhamun">Tutankhamun</a>. From the 18th century, European understanding of the colonies and biology of bees allowed the construction of the moveable comb hive so that honey could be harvested without destroying the colony.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Among Classical Era authors, beekeeping with the use of smoke is described in the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Animals" title="History of Animals">History of Animals</a></i> Book 9.<sup id="cite_ref-Aristotle_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The account mentions that bees die after stinging; that workers remove corpses from the hive, and guard it; castes including workers and non-working <a href="/wiki/Drone_(bee)" title="Drone (bee)">drones</a>, but "kings" rather than queens; predators including toads and bee-eaters; and the <a href="/wiki/Waggle_dance" title="Waggle dance">waggle dance</a>, with the "irresistible suggestion" of άpοσειονται (aroseiontai, it waggles) and παρακολουθούσιν (parakolouthousin, they watch).<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beekeeping is described in detail by <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Eclogues" title="Eclogues">Eclogues</a></i>; it is also mentioned in his <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, and in Pliny's <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_commercial_pollinators">As commercial pollinators</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_crop_plants_pollinated_by_bees" title="List of crop plants pollinated by bees">List of crop plants pollinated by bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollinator_decline" title="Pollinator decline">Pollinator decline</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_toxicity_to_bees" title="Pesticide toxicity to bees">Pesticide toxicity to bees</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG/220px-Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG/330px-Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG/440px-Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peponapis_pruinosaCane-12.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Squash_bee" title="Squash bee">Squash bees</a> (Apidae) are important pollinators of <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squashes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cucumber" title="Cucumber">cucumbers</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Bees play an important role in <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollinating</a> <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, and are the major type of <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinator</a> in many <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> that contain flowering plants. It is estimated that one third of the human food supply depends on <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> by insects, birds and bats, most of which is accomplished by bees, whether wild or domesticated.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Over the last half century, there has been a general decline in the species richness of wild bees and other pollinators, probably attributable to stress from increased parasites and disease, the use of pesticides, and a general decrease in the number of wild flowers. Climate change probably exacerbates the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pollination_management" title="Pollination management">Contract pollination</a> has overtaken the role of honey production for <a href="/wiki/Beekeeper" title="Beekeeper">beekeepers</a> in many countries. After the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Varroa_mites" class="mw-redirect" title="Varroa mites">Varroa mites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">feral</a> honey bees declined dramatically in the US, though their numbers have since recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The number of colonies kept by beekeepers declined slightly, through <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, systematic pesticide use, <a href="/wiki/Acarapis_woodi" title="Acarapis woodi">tracheal</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Varroa" title="Varroa">Varroa</a></i> mites, and the closure of beekeeping businesses. In 2006 and 2007 the rate of attrition increased, and was described as <a href="/wiki/Colony_collapse_disorder" title="Colony collapse disorder">colony collapse disorder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 invertebrate iridescent virus and the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Nosema_ceranae" title="Nosema ceranae">Nosema ceranae</a></i> were shown to be in every killed colony, and deadly in combination.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Winter losses increased to about 1/3.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <i>Varroa</i> mites were thought to be responsible for about half the losses.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from colony collapse disorder, losses outside the US have been attributed to causes including pesticide seed dressings, using <a href="/wiki/Neonicotinoid" title="Neonicotinoid">neonicotinoids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Clothianidin" title="Clothianidin">Clothianidin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imidacloprid" title="Imidacloprid">Imidacloprid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiamethoxam" title="Thiamethoxam">Thiamethoxam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> From 2013 the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> restricted some pesticides to stop bee populations from declining further.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> report warned that bees faced increased risk of extinction because of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In 2018 the European Union decided to ban field use of all three major neonicotinoids; they remain permitted in veterinary, greenhouse, and vehicle transport usage.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Farmers have focused on alternative solutions to mitigate these problems. By raising native plants, they provide food for native bee pollinators like <i><a href="/wiki/Lasioglossum_vierecki" title="Lasioglossum vierecki">Lasioglossum vierecki</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Farming_for_native_bees_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farming_for_native_bees-118">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lasioglossum_leucozonium" title="Lasioglossum leucozonium">L. leucozonium</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia-119">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> leading to less reliance on honey bee populations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food_producers">As food producers</span></h3>
<p>Honey is a natural product produced by bees and stored for their own use, but its sweetness has always appealed to humans. Before domestication of bees was even attempted, humans were raiding their nests for their honey. Smoke was often used to subdue the bees and such activities are depicted in <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">rock paintings</a> in Spain dated to 15,000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-EHSTMNWC_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHSTMNWC-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Honey bees are used commercially to produce <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> They also produce some substances used as dietary supplements with possible health benefits, pollen,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Propolis" title="Propolis">propolis</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_jelly" title="Royal jelly">royal jelly</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> though all of these can also cause <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergic reactions</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="As_food_.28bee_brood.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="As_food_(bee_brood)">As food (bee brood)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Botoktawon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Botoktawon.jpg/220px-Botoktawon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Botoktawon.jpg/330px-Botoktawon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Botoktawon.jpg/440px-Botoktawon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Botoktawon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bee larvae as food in the <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Javanese</a> dish <i><a href="/wiki/Botok" title="Botok">botok tawon</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Bees are partly considered <a href="/wiki/Edible_insects" class="mw-redirect" title="Edible insects">edible insects</a>. Indigenous people in many countries <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">eat insects</a>, including the larvae and pupae of bees, mostly stingless species. They also gather larvae, pupae and surrounding cells, known as <a href="/wiki/Bee_brood" title="Bee brood">bee brood</a>, for consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Holland2013_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holland2013-124">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> dish <i><a href="/wiki/Botok" title="Botok">botok tawon</a></i> from Central and East <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, bee larvae are eaten as a companion to <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, after being mixed with shredded <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconut</a>,  wrapped in <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">banana</a> leaves, and steamed.<sup id="cite_ref-Tasty2015_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tasty2015-125">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sayangi2015_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sayangi2015-126">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bee brood (pupae and larvae) although low in <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, has been found to be high in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a>, and a useful source of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, and trace minerals <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>. In addition, while bee brood was high in fat, it contained no fat soluble vitamins (such as A, D, and E) but it was a good source of most of the water-soluble <a href="/wiki/B-vitamins" class="mw-redirect" title="B-vitamins">B-vitamins</a> including <a href="/wiki/Choline" title="Choline">choline</a> as well as vitamin C. The fat was composed mostly of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fatty_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Monounsaturated fatty acids">monounsaturated fatty acids</a> with 2.0% being <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acids">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_alternative_medicine">As alternative medicine</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Apitherapy" title="Apitherapy">Apitherapy</a> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> that uses honey bee products, including <a href="/wiki/Raw_honey" class="mw-redirect" title="Raw honey">raw honey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_jelly" title="Royal jelly">royal jelly</a>, pollen, <a href="/wiki/Propolis" title="Propolis">propolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beeswax" title="Beeswax">beeswax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apitoxin" title="Apitoxin">apitoxin</a> (Bee <a href="/wiki/Venom" title="Venom">venom</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-MedicineWorld_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedicineWorld-129">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The claim that apitherapy treats cancer, which some proponents of apitherapy make, remains unsupported by <a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">evidence-based medicine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cass_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cass-130">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ades2009_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ades2009-131">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stings">Stings</span></h3>
<p>The painful <a href="/wiki/Bee_sting" title="Bee sting">stings of bees</a> are mostly associated with the poison gland and the <a href="/wiki/Dufour%27s_gland" title="Dufour&#39;s gland">Dufour's gland</a> which are abdominal exocrine glands containing various chemicals. In <i><a href="/wiki/Lasioglossum_leucozonium" title="Lasioglossum leucozonium">Lasioglossum leucozonium</a></i>, the Dufour's Gland mostly contains octadecanolide as well as some eicosanolide. There is also evidence of n-triscosane, n-<a href="/wiki/Heptacosane" class="mw-redirect" title="Heptacosane">heptacosane</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:4_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and 22-docosanolide.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-133">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> However, the secretions of these glands could also be used for nest construction.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-132">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In D'Arcy Thompson's translation: "At early dawn they make no noise, until some one particular bee makes a buzzing noise two or three times and thereby awakes the rest; hereupon they all fly in a body to work. By and by they return and at first are noisy; ... until at last some one bee flies round about, making a buzzing noise, and apparently calling on the others to go to sleep".<sup id="cite_ref-Aristotle_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
